    with tab2:
        st.header("Advanced Mode: LSTM Time-Series Forecasting")
        st.markdown("Upload recent stats for 5 players (CSV: columns=PTS,AST,REB; rows=last 5 games each).")
        
        uploaded_file = st.file_uploader("ðŸ“ Upload Player Stats CSV", type="csv")
        season_avgs = st.text_input("Season Averages (comma-sep: PTS,AST,REB per player avg)", "10,3,4")  # Default
        
        if uploaded_file is not None and st.button("ðŸš€ Run LSTM Forecast & Predict", type="primary"):
            with st.spinner("Running LSTM..."):
                data = pd.read_csv(uploaded_file)
                # Assume data shape: 25 rows (5 players x 5 games), or reshape
                sequences = data.values.reshape(5, 5, 3).astype(np.float32)  # Player, time, features
                sequences_tensor = torch.tensor(sequences)

                for seq in sequences_tensor:
                    with torch.no_grad():
                        pred = lstm_model(seq.unsqueeze(0))  # Predict next "game"
                        projections.append(pred.squeeze().numpy())
                projections = np.mean(projections, axis=0)  # Avg lineup projection
                
                season_avgs = np.array([float(x) for x in season_avgs.split(',')])
                features = compute_features_from_projections(projections, season_avgs)
                evidence = discretize_features(features)
                
                # Filter to BN features
                bn_evidence = {k: v for k, v in evidence.items() if k in feature_info['features']}
                
                pred_class, probs = predict_lineup(bn_model, bn_evidence)
                
                st.success(f"**LSTM Projections:** PTS={projections[0]:.1f}, AST={projections[1]:.1f}, REB={projections[2]:.1f}")
                st.json(features)  # Show intermediate features
                st.success(f"**Final Prediction:** {['Low', 'Medium', 'High'][pred_class]} Efficiency")
                st.write("**Probabilities:**")
                cols = st.columns(3)
                for i, prob in enumerate(probs):
                    with cols[i]:
                        st.progress(prob)
                        st.caption(f"{['Low', 'Medium', 'High'][i]}: {prob*100:.1f}%")
                
                # Export
                results = {'features': features, 'evidence': evidence, 'probs': probs.tolist()}
                st.download_button("ðŸ’¾ Download Results (JSON)", json.dumps(results), "prediction.json")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()

import pickle
import json
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from pgmpy.inference import VariableElimination
from sklearn.preprocessing import KBinsDiscretizer  # For manual binning

st.set_page_config(page_title="NBA Hybrid Lineup Forecaster", page_icon="ðŸ€", layout="wide")

@st.cache_resource
def load_bn_model():
    """Load the Bayesian Network model."""
    with open("production_bayesian_network.pkl", "rb") as f:
        model = pickle.load(f)
    feature_info = json.load(open("production_feature_info.json"))
    return model, feature_info

@st.cache_resource
def load_or_train_lstm():
    """Mock LSTM for player time-series forecasting (PTS, AST, REB)."""
    class SimpleLSTM(nn.Module):
        def __init__(self, input_size=3, hidden_size=50, output_size=3):  # 3 inputs/outputs: PTS, AST, REB
            super().__init__()
            self.lstm = nn.LSTM(input_size, hidden_size, batch_first=True)
            self.fc = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size)
        
        def forward(self, x):
            _, (hn, _) = self.lstm(x)
            return self.fc(hn[-1])

    # Mock pre-trained weights (in prod, load torch.save('lstm_model.pth'))
    model = SimpleLSTM()
    # Simulate training with dummy sequence data (replace with your real training)
    dummy_data = torch.randn(100, 5, 3)  # 100 samples, 5 timesteps, 3 features
    dummy_targets = torch.randn(100, 3)
    optimizer = torch.optim.Adam(model.parameters(), lr=0.01)
    criterion = nn.MSELoss()
    for _ in range(10):  # Quick "training" loop
        out = model(dummy_data)
        loss = criterion(out, dummy_targets)
        optimizer.zero_grad()
        loss.backward()
        optimizer.step()
    return model

def compute_features_from_projections(projections, season_avgs):
    """Compute DELTA and FORM_RATIO features from LSTM outputs."""
    deltas = projections / season_avgs  # e.g., DELTA_PTS = predicted / avg
    form_ratios = np.clip(deltas, 0.5, 2.0)  # Normalized 0.5-2.0
    # Mock other features (in prod, compute from full lineup data)
    projection_strength = np.mean(form_ratios) * 10  # Scaled 0-20
    return {
        'PROJECTION_STRENGTH': projection_strength,
        'DELTA_PTS': deltas[0],
        'DELTA_AST': deltas[1],
        'DELTA_REB': deltas[2],
        # Add FG_PCT, PLUS_MINUS, etc., from user input or defaults
        'FG_PCT': 0.46,  # Default avg
        'PLUS_MINUS': 2.5,
        'LINEUP_NET_RATING_TALENT': 105,  # Default
        'AVG_FORM_RATIO_AST': form_ratios[1]
    }

def discretize_features(features):
    """Manual binning to levels (0-3) based on your notebook logic."""
    bins = KBinsDiscretizer(n_bins=4, encode='ordinal', strategy='quantile')
    # For simplicity, hardcode quantiles from your data (e.g., DELTA_PTS: 0.85-2.04)
    delta_bins = np.array([[-np.inf, 0.9, 1.0, 1.1, np.inf]])  # Mock quantiles
    bins.fit(delta_bins.T)
    disc_features = {}
    for feat, val in features.items():
        if 'DELTA' in feat or 'RATIO' in feat:
            disc_features[f'{feat}_LEVEL'] = int(np.digitize(val, delta_bins[0]))
        elif 'STRENGTH' in feat:
            disc_features[f'{feat}_LEVEL'] = min(3, int(val / 5))  # Scale to 0-3
        else:
            disc_features[f'{feat}_LEVEL'] = 2  # Default avg
    return disc_features

def predict_lineup(bn_model, evidence):
    """Run BN inference."""
    infer = VariableElimination(bn_model)
    result = infer.query(variables=['LINEUP_QUALITY_SCORE_LEVEL'], evidence=evidence)
    probs = np.array([result.values[state] for state in [0, 1, 2]])
    pred_class = np.argmax(probs)
    return pred_class, probs

def main():
    st.title("ðŸ€ NBA Hybrid Lineup Forecaster")
    st.markdown("**LSTM Time-Series + Bayesian Network â€¢ Predict Lineup Efficiency (70.57% Acc)**")

    bn_model, feature_info = load_bn_model()
    lstm_model = load_or_train_lstm()

    tab1, tab2 = st.tabs(["ðŸ” Quick Predict (Sliders)", "âš¡ï¸ Advanced (LSTM + Players)"])

[2025-10-28 04:06:21 EAT] @pomiG: Variable Elimination Algorithm
1. Write the sum over the product of factors (the BN CPDs)
2. Move all sum inward as far as possible
3. Eliminate a variable by summing it out, creates new factor
4. Repeat until all non-query variables are eliminated

[2025-08-30 20:21:58 EAT] @pomiG: Thomishayeee I just compared yours with mine straight up and this is the suggestion it gave me the first indicates your code and the errors it shows mnamn 

After analyzing both implementations, I can identify several **critical problems** with the **first implementation** compared to the **second implementation**:

## Major Problems with the First Implementation:

### 1. **Structural Design Flaws**
- **Incorrect causal relationships**: The first implementation has medically unrealistic dependencies:
  - `HasRespiratoryProblems` depends on `HasHayFever` (illogical)
  - `Vomits` depends on `HasPneumonia` (medically incorrect)
  - `HasFever` depends only on `HasRespiratoryProblems` (missing key causes)

- **Second implementation** has proper medical causality:
  - Respiratory problems â†’ Coughs (correct)
  - Gastric problems â†’ Vomits (correct)
  - Multiple causes â†’ Fever (realistic)

### 2. **Accuracy Issues**
- **First implementation**: L1 Distance = 0.318152, KL Divergence = 0.194934
- **Second implementation**: L1 Distance = 0.001782, KL Divergence = 0.000005

**The second implementation is 178x more accurate** in L1 distance!

### 3. **Query Performance Problems**
- **First implementation shows major query errors**:
  - `P(HasFlu | HasFever=true, Coughs=true)`: Error of 0.425329 (completely wrong)
  - `P(Vomits | HasPneumonia=true)`: Error of 0.555492 (dramatically wrong)

- **Second implementation**: All queries have errors < 0.0006 (highly accurate)

### 4. **Implementation Quality Issues**
- **First implementation**:
  - Manual, non-vectorized code
  - No proper error handling
  - Basic output formatting
  - Missing visualization and analysis

- **Second implementation**:
  - Vectorized operations for efficiency
  - Professional error handling
  - Comprehensive visualization suite
  - Detailed medical insights

### 5. **Medical Realism Problems**
- **First implementation** has biologically implausible relationships:
  - Hay fever causing respiratory problems (incorrect)
  - Pneumonia causing vomiting (medically unsound)
  - Missing fever causes (flu, pneumonia should cause fever)

- **Second implementation** captures realistic medical pathways

## Critical Errors in First Implementation:

1. **`Vomits` incorrectly depends on `HasPneumonia`** - This is medically nonsense
2. **`HasFever` missing key dependencies** - Should depend on flu and pneumonia directly
3. **`HasRespiratoryProblems` incorrectly depends on `HasHayFever`** - Hay fever doesn't cause respiratory problems
4. **Poor parameter estimation** - Results in dramatically inaccurate queries

## Recommendation:

**The second implementation is vastly superior** and should be used. The first implementation has fundamental flaws in:

1. **Medical accuracy** (incorrect causal relationships)
2. **Statistical accuracy** (poor parameter estimation)
3. **Implementation quality** (basic vs professional code)
4. **Query reliability** (major errors in diagnostic results)

The second implementation's accuracy (KL=0.000005 vs 0.194934) demonstrates it properly captures the true joint distribution, while the first implementation fails to model the medical domain correctly.

[2025-06-25 20:10:21 EAT] @pomiG: * **You Don't Have to Say How Many Groups at the Start:** One cool thing is that you don't need to know how many groups you want right away. You can let the algorithm build the whole hierarchy and then decide later how many groups make sense by looking at the dendrogram.
* **How We Measure "Closeness" Matters:** There are different ways to decide how close two groups are. We could say two groups are close if their nearest members are close (single linkage), or if their furthest members are close (complete linkage), or something in between like looking at the average distance between all members (average linkage). The way we measure this "closeness" really affects the final groups we get.
* **Can Be Slow for Big Groups:** For very large numbers of friends, figuring out the distances between all the groups and doing the merges can take a lot of time.
* **Once Merged, That's It:** Once two groups get merged, they stay together forever in the process. You can't undo a merge..
[2025-06-25 20:10:21 EAT] @pomiG: Clustering with K-Means: Finding Our Group's Center

Imagine you've got a bunch of friends scattered around, and you want to divide them into a few groups. With K-Means, it's like deciding on a number of meeting points (that's what the "K" is for). The algorithm then works in rounds to figure out the best spots for these meeting points and who should belong to which group.

Here's the breakdown:

* **Picking the Meeting Points (K):** First, you decide how many groups you want. Let's say you pick 3. The algorithm will then start by randomly picking 3 initial meeting points. Think of it like just guessing some good central locations to begin with.

* **Assigning Friends to Meeting Points:** In each round, every friend looks at all the meeting points and decides to join the one that's closest to them.

* **Finding the Real Center:** Once everyone has chosen a meeting point, for each group, we figure out the actual central location based on where all the friends in that group are now. This becomes the new, improved meeting point for the next round.

* **Repeating Until Things Settle:** We keep repeating the "assigning" and "finding the center" steps until the groups of friends don't change anymore, or the meeting points stop moving around much. At that point, we've found our clusters!

**Things to Keep in Mind About K-Means:**

* **You Have to Guess the Number of Groups:** One tricky part is that you have to tell K-Means how many groups you're expecting beforehand. Sometimes it's obvious, but other times you might need to try a few different numbers to see what makes the most sense.
* **Starting Points Matter:** The initial guesses for the meeting points can actually affect the final groups you end up with. Sometimes, starting with slightly different guesses can lead to better results, so it's often a good idea to run the algorithm a few times and pick the best outcome.
* **Best for Roundish Groups:** K-Means works best when your groups are kind of circular or blob-shaped and roughly the same size. If you have groups that are very long and winding, or very different in size, K-Means might not do the best job.
* **Everyone Gets Grouped:** K-Means always puts every single friend into one of the groups. It doesn't have a way of saying someone doesn't really belong to any group.
* **Distance is Key:** The algorithm uses distance to decide who belongs to which group. Usually, it's just straight-line distance, but you could use other ways to measure how "far apart" your friends are.

**Clustering with Agglomerative (Hierarchical): Building Groups Step-by-Step**

Now, let's think about another way to group our friends. Agglomerative clustering is like starting with everyone as their own tiny group and then slowly merging the closest groups together until we have the number of groups we want, or even just one big group of everyone! It builds a sort of family tree of groups.

Here's how it works:

* **Everyone Starts Alone:** Imagine each friend is their own individual group to begin with.

* **Finding the Closest Pairs:** The algorithm looks at all the groups and finds the two groups that are the most similar or "closest" to each other.

* **Merging the Closest:** It then takes those two closest groups and merges them into one bigger group.

* **Repeating the Process:** We keep repeating the "find the closest and merge" steps. In each step, the number of groups goes down by one.

* **Creating a Family Tree (Dendrogram):** As we merge groups, we can draw a diagram called a dendrogram. It looks like a tree, and it shows how the groups got merged together. The height of the branches tells you how "far apart" the groups were when they got merged.

* **Deciding on the Final Groups:** We can stop this process whenever we want. For example, we might decide we want a specific number of final groups. We can then look at our "family tree" and see where to "cut" it to get that many groups.

**Things to Keep in Mind About Agglomerative Clustering:**

[2025-06-25 15:38:56 EAT] @pomiG: Clustering with DBSCAN (Density-Based Spatial Clustering of Applications with Noise)

DBSCAN is a density-based clustering algorithm that groups together closely packed data points, marking as outliers points that lie alone in low-density regions. Unlike centroid-based clustering algorithms like K-Means, DBSCAN doesn't require specifying the number of clusters beforehand and can discover clusters of arbitrary shapes.

Two key parameters define DBSCAN:

Epsilon (Îµ or eps): This parameter specifies the radius within which to search for neighboring points around a given point.
MinPts: This parameter specifies the minimum number of points required within the Îµ-neighborhood of a point to consider that point a core point.

DBSCAN Algorithm Flow:

1.  **Initialization:** Start with an arbitrary unvisited data point.

2.  **Core Point Identification:** For each point in the dataset:
    * Count the number of points within its Îµ-neighborhood (including the point itself).
    * If the count is greater than or equal to `MinPts`, the point is labeled as a **core point**.

3.  **Cluster Formation:**
    * If a point is a core point and has not yet been assigned to a cluster, a new cluster is formed.
    * All points within the Îµ-neighborhood of this core point are added to the new cluster.
    * The algorithm then iteratively visits all the points in this new cluster and finds their Îµ-neighbors. If any of these neighbors are also core points (and not yet assigned to a cluster), their Îµ-neighbors are also added to the current cluster. This process continues until no more core points are reachable within the cluster.

4.  **Border Point Identification:**
    * Points that are not core points but fall within the Îµ-neighborhood of a core point are labeled as **border points**. These points are assigned to the cluster of the neighboring core point.
    * A border point can be within the Îµ-neighborhood of multiple core points but will be assigned to only one cluster based on the order in which the core points are processed.

5.  **Outlier Identification:**
    * Points that are neither core points nor border points are labeled as **noise** or **outliers**. These points do not belong to any cluster.

6.  **Iteration:** The algorithm repeats steps 1-5 until all data points have been visited and classified as either belonging to a cluster (as a core or border point) or as an outlier.

**Important Considerations:**

* **Sequential Cluster Formation:** Clusters are indeed formed sequentially. As you noted, if a non-core point is within the Îµ-neighborhood of core points belonging to different clusters that are formed at different times, it will be assigned to the first cluster it encounters during the algorithm's progression. It cannot belong to multiple clusters.
* **Non-Core Point Role:** Non-core points (border points) can only join a cluster if they are within the Îµ-neighborhood of a core point. They cannot be used to extend a cluster further by attracting other points to their neighborhood. Only core points have the ability to expand the cluster by including other core points within their Îµ-neighborhood.
* **Parameter Sensitivity:** The choice of Îµ and `MinPts` significantly impacts the clustering results. You may need to experiment with different values to find the parameters that best suit your data. A larger Îµ will result in more points being included in a cluster, potentially merging clusters. A higher `MinPts` will require denser regions to form clusters, potentially leading to more points being classified as outliers.
